THE JERUSALEM FAIR
The Jacksonville Jewish Center, in celebration of
the festival of Hanukah, transformed its building and
exterior to recreate the mood of the City of Jerusalem.
Upon entering this model city through the “Jaffa Gate”,
one entered an old recreated street covered with arches
reminscent of the old City of Jerusalem which leads to
thw Western Wall. As one traversed the streets of this
model of the ancient city, sacred to Jews and Christians
during this holiday season, one passed through
courtyards and bazaars lined with shops, which convey
the sense and atmosphere of Jerusalem. An Israeli
marketplace, bustling with flowers, Jaffa oranges, quaint
shops and stores carries as well the aroma of the foods of
Israel. An Israeli cinema featured the films of Israel and a
display of the history of this ancient city, emphasizing the
historic character of a city sacred to three major
religions, is within view.
Tower of David being hoisted by youths and adults of the
Center.
Andrew Hoppen stands guard at Jaffa Gate.
The Jerusalem Marketplace featured Mid-East
merchandise for shoppers and onlookers.
Archeological dig was supervised by Lee Hoppen.
Ancient coins and pottery were discovered.
Tabooli makers L-R: Lee Hoppen, JoAnne Davis, Judy Silverman, Linda Levy,
Sonja Morris. Not pictured - Felafel cooks: Perla Graff and Alina Kentof.

===== PAGE 12 =====
HA* RAYIM
What do wine and cheese parties, picnics, softball,
backjack and friendship have to do with Ha’Rayim?
Everything!
This year is going to be our most exciting ever. Several of our new members
have brought fresh ideas and enthusiasm to our group. For instance, this
year we have already had a picnic, a roller skating party, and a Luau
at the Center pool. Among the future events planned are tubing down the Ichitucknee, a camp-out,
a wine and cheese party, and to top off a fabulous year, a gala New Year’s Eve
Party.
This is just a sample of what Ra’Rayim did last year. We are a group of
Jewish couples who enjoy each other’s company. When we all get
together, we always have fun regardless of what we do.
We are not a fund raising organization. Our
goal is to provide a pleasant environment
where Jewish couples can get together and
have an enjoyable time for a reasonable
amount. Please join us for our next function.
There will be one soon!
Ha’Rayim Presidents
Bonnie and Neil Sandler
Fjauira Qa Misbpacba
Havurat Ha-Mishpacha, a group of young Center families who have organized to plan
religious, educational and cultural experiences, have planned a very exciting year. The
year began with a Shabbat experience. The group met for afternoon Minha services and
then assembled for a tasteful Shalosh Seudot filled with singing of old and new Israeli and
Hassidic songs. The program concluded with a very beautiful and creative Havdalah and
a pleasant exchange of Shavua-tov, a good week. Any young families interested in inten¬
sifying their Jewish commitments and personal growtK through education, religious ob¬
servances and Jewish experiences please feel free to participate.
An open invitation is being extended to all young families. You may call the Center office,
Rabbi David Gaffney or Dr. Neil Abramson for more information.

===== PAGE 22 =====
NATIONAL
COUNCIL OF JEWISH WOMEN
EDUCATION SOCIAL ACTION SERVICE
OFFICERS:
President Thelma Nied
Vice-Presidents Carolyn Krestul
Dorothy Shlafer
Corresponding Secretary Betty Rohan
Recording Secretary Susan Piha
Financial Secretary JoyKorman
Treasurer Barbara Safer
The National Council of Jewish Women is a volunteer
organization dedicated in the spirit of Judaism to advancing
human welfare and the democratic way of life. Through a
coordinated program of education, service, advocacy and
social action, NCJW works in the Jewish and general
communities, locally, nationally and internationally.
Our local projects include: Preschool vision testing to
discover sight problems, service to the foreign born, Art
Enrichment Program (“Picture Lady”) which trains
volunteers at the Art Museum to bring and discuss art in the
public and private schools. Our Mental Health Forum, co-
sponsered with the Mental Health Association and Florida
Junior College, attracted over 2000 participants.
Our newest project, CASA (Court Appointed Special
Advocates), trains volunteers to act as a “friend of the
court” in the best interest of an abused or negleted child. A
CASA is a guardian ad litem in accordance with Florida law.
Each CASA attends two training sessions, three hours
each, conducted by professionals in the field. In addition,
on-going educations programs will be offered by the staff,
and legal counsel will be available to provide advice and
assistance, and, if necessary, to appear in court with the
CASA. For further information and application forms, call
the program office, 633-3709.
NCJW’s commitment to Israel dates back to the years
before there was an Israel, to the years when Jews around
the world were working hand in hand to establish a Jewish
state. NCJW took an active role during those years. At the
UN, NCJW’s representative worked with single-minded
devotion to help ensure that there would be such a state. In
Jerusalem, NCJW helped the Hebrew University establish
programs which would ensure that the new nation would
have the foundation upon which to develop a stron network
of educational resources. And in the United States, NCJW
worked to develop understanding and support for the state-
to-be.
Throughout the intervening years, NCJW’s
determination to help build a strong fabric of life in Israel,
while ensuring its security as a nation, has never wavered.
Within Israel NCJW’s strong programs in education and
social welfare have helped to develop the country’s
leadership.
For membership information, please contact any of
our officers.

Jacksonville Hevrah Kaddisha
JOEL BRESSLER
President
Communal fraternities for the purpose of providing
burial and its attendant rites for the dead have existed
among the Jews ever since the Talmudic period. The
Hebrew term for such a fraternity is Hevrah Kaddisha (Holy
Society).
As soon as the society is notified by the local funeral
home of a Jewish death, out of respect for the dead, a
Shomer or watchman is notified and sits with the body,
especially at night, until the time of the funeral.
The practices of cremation, speeding up the
decomposition of the body, or embalming, the slowing
down the process, flowers at the funeral or public viewing of
the body are all discouraged by Jewish custom both
universally and locally.
The Jacksonville Hevrah Kaddish was formed in
January, 1974, and has been functioning ever since. It
consists of about twenty men and women who perform the
necessary tasks on a rotating basis. Anyone who would be
interested in becoming a member of the local society may
contact Joel Bressler, its President, for further information.
History and Activities of Hevrah Kaddisha
The members of the burial society used to attend at the
bedside of the dying person and receive his final declaration
or confession of faith (Viddui). They would wash and dress
the corpse, conduct the burial service, and finally, together
with the observance and performance of the traditional
rites, conduct the actual burial itself. In the case of the poor,
the burial society performed its services without any
charge. In all cases, however, whether there is a charge or
not, the members of the society fulfill their task as a sacred
duty or obligation (Mitzvah) which confers merit upon all
who take part therein. Since respect and acts of charity
shown to the dead were regarded by the Jews in all ages as
the highest form of charity and human service, the
members of the Hevrah Kaddisha have always been highly
respected in the Jewish communities; in many cases in the
past they were accorded special social and religious
privileges in return for their important and unselfish service.
The actual preparation of the dead for burial,
undertaken by the Hevrah Kaddisha, is known in Jewish
law as “taharah” (cleansing, purifying). During this Taharah
definitely prescribed ceremonies are observed,
accompanied by the reading of specific prayers. The body is
washed from head to foot in lukewarm water, dried and
then clad in the burial clothes (Tachrichin). During all this
ceremony the members of the society preserve a proper
gravity and respect. To prevent funerals from becoming
very expensive burdens upon the survivors Rabbon
Gamaliel 1, in the latter part of the First Century, ordered
that he be buried in a simple linen garment, and this
procedure was soon adopted by the great masses of the
Jewish people. Men are in addition wrapped in the same
Tallit (prayer shawl) which they have worn at services
during their life but with one of the fringes removed. As soon
as the body is fully clothed in the Tachrichin, it is placed in a
simple wooden coffin, again in an effort to provide
uniformity in the treatment of the dead and to prevent
excessive cost of burial.
If one wishes to honor their dead by purchasing a more
expensive casket, they are encouraged instead to give
the additional money to the deceased’s favorite loved one’s
charity.

Solomon Schechter Day School
r
Let me share with you some encouraging and noteworthy facts
concerning the current school year. First and foremost, we have been
blessed with a major increase in pupil enrollment, which includes two year
olds through the sixth grade. We are equally fortunate to have on our staff
sixteen very fine teachers, each of whom is contributing in an outstanding
manner to the development and education of our children.
At this point in time, we can all be very optimistic concerning the viability
and future prospects of the Day School.
As we continue to grow, however, it is important to keep in sharp focus
the reasons that led to the establishment of the Day School, for those are the
very same factors that sustain it today. Only in a day school can in-depth
Jewish education be imparted in an integrated and natural manner. In this
setting, identity and commitment to Jewish life can be instilled.
A fine general education can be assured through the efforts of talented
and dedicated and typically small class sizes which allow for individual needs.
However, it is the substantial Jewish content — basic texts, traditions,
observances, values, and Hebrew language that makes the day school as an
institution unique.
During the past year, an Extended Care Program (12:00-3:00 care for 3
year olds - 5 year olds) was started and proved very successful. It gave work¬
ing parents aid as well as it gave many other mothers a “Mother’s Afternoon
Out.”
We are very proud of the many accomplishments that are taking place
within the walls of our Solomon Schechter Day School. Marilyn Sandler

The Afternoon Hebrew School
The Afternoon Hebrew School of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is designed for students in grades 3 - 7 and provides an
elementary education leading to Bar/Bat Mitzvah and continuation in the high school program.
Meeting twice during the week and on Saturdays, “the basics” are at the core of the curriculum, with stress on Hebrew Reading,
Jewish Social Studies and current events relating to Israel are discussed throughly. Synagogue skills, holiday observances and
knowledge of prayers are all part of the comprehensive afternoon Hebrew School program.

Daily JI\ii?yai7
The Jacksonville Jewish Center takes pride in its
Daily Minyan. Through the tireless efforts of the cap¬
tains and co-captains, we have minyans every morning
and evening.
The anniversary date of the passing of a loved one
presents an opportunity to observe a Mitzvah, if not
a double Mitzvah.
The Mitzvah of observing the Yahrzeit is obvious -
the second Mitzvah is the opportunity to insure a minyan
so that others, besides yourself, can also observe or
attend a service for their own reasons.
The Daily Minyan needs you. For a satisfying and
rewarding experience, attend once or twice a month on
a regular basis.
If you plan to attend, call the captain of the day and
time.
Sunday - morning Philip Selber; evening Nathan
Shmunes, Bill Goldberg
Monday - morning Allen Gray; evening Nathan
Franzblau, Jeffery Morris
Tuesday - morning Ben Edwards, Louis Slott;
evening, Martin Brauner, Dr. Michael
Greenburg
Wednesday morning Nathan Friedlin; evening Jack
Silverman
Thursday - morning Norman Moss; evening Sol
Proctor
Friday - morning Sonny D. Sandler; evening Philip
Selber
Philip
(Excerpts from comments made by
Rabbi Gaffney at the funeral of Philip Bork,
past President of the Jacksonville Jewish Center)
Jewish tradition harbors a sentiment first expressed
in the life of our forefather, Jacob, which has been
re-enacted innumerable times in the lives of noble
people .. . re-enacted in the life and death of Philip Bork.
In this tradition as the rabbis perceive it, Jacob’s depart¬
ure from the city of his birth is described in great detail
because the Bible would have us observe that the depart¬
ure of a noble man leaves his family, community and
circle of friends impoverished by his absence. What
after all is a human community but the sum total of
what its individual members bring to it? If this is so, we
must recognize that certain individuals bring more than
others. Consequently their departure leaves our com¬
munity weakened.
In this regard, we say to you Philip, Lech I’shalom,
“Go in peace”. Your parting has touched us not only
with regard to the fragile nature of our mortality but also
with regard to the fragile nature of our social fabric. With
(Cont. from page 39)
the Aged, and has served as a vice-
chairman for the Duval County
Committee on Aging. Sheldon has
not only expressed his love for Ju¬
daism in institutional settings but
has brought its values to shape his
noble and virtuous character. He
has been an example of strength
and encouragement to his family
and friends and has always proven
to be a thoughtful and sympathetic
soul. The sight of Sheldon, his wife
Horty and family, seated in a fami¬
liar setting on the left hand side of
the synagogue every Shabbat is a
testimony to what a Jewish leader
really is.
Bork
your déath a chapter is closed on our collective lives and
we recognize that we are now weakened and diminished.
Philip Bork was a gifted man. He was gifted not only
in what he had, but what he did, not only in what he
received, but what he gave. He was gifted not because
of what he won from life, but what he brought to it.
He recognized that man does not live by bread
alone. He lived with conscience and so Philip was
touched by the need to bring justice to the world and
equity to our people. He was one of the founders of the
Zionist chapter in our community, an organizer of Men’s
ORT and a chairman for Israel Bonds. He also recogniz¬
ed that man is a believer and so he was instrumental in
creating a vital congregation that became the Jackson¬
ville Jewish Center, and he served auspiciously as its
President.
He was also gifted in the personal affection, love and
companionship that he had known and that he was able
to leave through his deeds, monuments more enduring
than life and time itself. May his soul be bound up in the
bonds of eternal life.
• Observe their Yahrzeit by attending the
Daily Minyan.

Thank you friends for making this yearbook possible, by your participation
and financial support of the Center, we are able to bring to you and your
families, the finest programs, activities and spiritual fulfillment you need.
My sincere thanks to Irvin Ghelerter, Barbara Safer and Hymie Selber
for their encouragement and help. To our own Blanche Slott, who is always
there to share her talents, I am grateful for the clever community title page
and the attractive Greetings cover.
And to Raymond Cohen and his daughter, Peggy Organes, a Yasher
Koach for the beautiful cover design. Rabbi Gaffney’s message gives an
explicit description of the Hallel wall, which will appear in our lobby. To Don
Morris, our photographer, who donates his time and equipment, we are
extremely grateful.
A happy and healthy New Year to all of you.
Isabel Balotin

GIRL SCOUT TROOP #162
This has been a fun filled and exciting year for our troop. We had
two campouts during the year, where we learned to pitch our own
tents and cook over an open fire. We put on a play about Juliette
Low the evening of our investiture. We had a sleepover at Clara
Mann’s beach house. Our first annual talent show was a smashing
success.
We also spent the weekend in Ocala for a Girl Scout Weekend
at Wild Waters. During the year we learned a variety of arts
and crafts such as making a magnet that resembled a female
pilgrim and making flowers out of satin ribbons.
The Troop also learned about good grooming techniques from
Marilyn Anderson, a troop mother who is a hair stylist in the area,
and through a drawing, presented some of the girls with prizes that
included a curling iron, hot rollers and brushes. Additionally,
Karen Nathanson, another troop mother presented each girl with
a “grab bag” of grooming tools. In between trips to the Museum
and Planetarium, our girls did exceptionally well in the annual
cookie drive.
This has been a very exciting year for our troop, and we have
expanded from seven girls to nineteen! Anyone interested in
joining our troop, contact the Center, Cookie Taussig, 268-4469;
Clara Mann, 733-6472; or Nancy Wall, 268-8211.
Cookie Taussig

sent the Worlds of Well-Being, Out of Doors, Today and Tomorrow, Arts and People. This provided
for a well-rounded Brownie program.
If your daughter is between 7 and 9 years of age and is interested in Brownies, call Sheila Baruch, 733-0086.

MR. & MRS. CLUB
OFFICERS BOARD MEMBERS
President Harry & Lilo Frisch Gus& Edith Gross Joe & Roslyn Klein

Recording Secretary Maurice & Estelle Cherry
Ex-offico Paul & Esther Fox
Boasting of the longest continuous existence of any social club in the Jewish community, the member¬
ship of the Mr. and Mrs. Club consists of just what its name implies. It was organized a quarter of a century
ago for the sole purpose of promoting fellowship, friendships and good times for married Jewish couples.
Dances are held every month to the music of live bands. Meals and snacks are served and each com¬
mittee decides on its own type of entertainment. All of this is climaxed with a gala New Year’s dance
including a cocktail party, full course dinner and professional entertainment.
As this organization is non-profit, all of the aforementioned is enjoyed at a nominal cost. There is no
age limit and membership is open to the entire married Jewish Community. Couples who are interested in
joining may contact any officer or board member.

KADIMA CONVENTIONS
Approximately 250 young people converged upon
the Jacksonville Jewish Center on February 8th - 10th
for our second annual Kadima convention. There was
a wealth of activities for all, including a song and dance
festival that was held during the weekend. The over-all
theme of the convention was: “The Many Faces of
Judaism,” which dealt with the four movements in
Judaism. This material was explored at the study groups
schedule throughout the weekend. This was rounded
out with athletics, a dynamic disco-social, and fire sign.
Jacksonville Jewish Center Kadima
Wendy Kesler & Holly Sandler
are all smiles!
Kadima advisors take a break.
The bus is a great place to sleep...
Sabrina Katz & Judith Abramson
exhausted after a weekend
convention.
Sabrina Katz holds “Ruach Award”
that was won by the Jacksonville
Kadima at the Atlanta Kadima
Convention.
The worst part of a
convention is saying
“goodbye.”
Gary Manasse, Jonathan Gray
& Ozzie Schindler enjoy breakfast.

rinyanaire
Minyanaire Machers
Abe Bielski, Jay Graff, Neil Sandler
Gene Kornblum, Allen Grey, Matt Frankel
WHO ARE THE
MINYANAIRES?
WHAT IS THE
MINYANAIRE
PROGRAM?
Minyanaires are men and women of all ages and life styles who attend one, some or every
Minyanaire Program.
The Minyanaire Program is held most Sundays at the Jacksonville Jewish Center. It begins
at 9 a.m. with a service held in the intimate atmosphere of the Witten Chapel. This is followed
by breakfast and the speaker of the day. We feature speakers on a variety of subjects that will
inform, excite, or entertain you. All Minyanaire Programs are free of charge.
WHY DO
MINYANAIRES
ATTEND?
WHO DO WE
NEED?
Minyanaires attend for various reasons. The brief service is inspirational. The friendship and
comraderie felt at breakfast is a pleasant change of pace from our usual morning routine. It’s
an opportunity to see old friends and meet new ones. There is no membership or on-going
obligation. Some may choose only a few programs to attend—some are there every week.
YOU!
We cordially invite you to try the Center’s Minyanaire Program. For more information call
Abe Bielski—733-7430, Matt Frankel—737-9525, or Arlene Zel—733-4955.
A Babysitter is available during all Minyanaire Programs.
***SPECIAL NOTE to new or inactive Center members ... If you would like to become
invloved in any aspect of THE MINYANAIRE PROGRAM, we would love to have you on our
committee. Just call Abe, Matt, or Arlene and say, “I’ll help!”
BURIAL OF SACRED BOOKS
All members of the congregation, including U.S.Y.ers attending a
convention at the Center, participated in a Congregational Burial of
Ritual Objects and Sacred Books on November 18, 1979.
Jewish Law requires that worn out objects such as prayerbooks,
mezuzot, tefillin, taleism, Hebrew books and other Sacred volumes
must be treated reverently and therefore must be interred when
they are no longer serviceable.
This was a wonderful opportunity for both children and adults to
become acquainted with a hallowed Jewish practice which occurs
very infrequently.
Rabbi Gaffney throws the first shovel of dirt on books.
Many men and women
participated in this unique
U.S.Y. President Barry
Ansbacher was a big help.
Rabbi Gaffney explains the importance of the
Book Burial to Seth Rothstein & other Day School
Students.

PROFILE
HARRY GENDZIER
"A Man For All Seasons”
"To be a happy and worthwhile
individual, you must share your life
with others” This idea was taught
to Harry Gendzier by his mother and
has guided him in his dedication to
the Jewish Community. To under¬
stand Harry, one needs a little
background sketch of this unique
character.
The year was 1916 and war raged.
In the deserts of Palestine,
Britons, Arabs and Turks were
waging sporadic war over a land once
ruled by biblical kings.
In the trenches of Russia, Jews
were dying, sometimes at the hands
of attacking Germans, sometimes at
the hands of their Russian
countrymen.
And in New York City, 19 year old
Harry Gendzier, inflamed with
pride, anger and hope, was writing
an impassioned story he called,
"Awake, O, Israel."
"Pack your cudgels ye sons of
Maccabees, falter not," wrote
Gendzier. "Live and hope ... but then
work . . . send this message to your
brethren across the sea. Make of our
race as it was before The Race.
Jerusalem, let us meet there, United
and Peace Loving. Shalom, Shalom."
That was 32 years before a State of
Israel existed and 52 years before
Harry and his wife, Belle, first saw
the promised land.
AN ACTIVE YOUTH
Growing up in New York City,
Harry was a familiar figure around
the Peter Cooper Club, an
Educational Alliance and Jewish
Settlement house that a fellow
named Eddie Cantor spent a lot of
time in, and a youngster named
David Sarnoff. At a young age, Harry
became interested in the Zionist
Youth groups and that is when he
wrote the aforementioned article
which was published in several
magazines, including Jacksonville’s
first Jewish Publication, "Spring
Festival."
Leaving New York, Harry came by
boat to Jacksonville in the fall of
1917, planning to enroll in law
school in Gainesville. He learned
classes would not start for several
months so he went to work as a shoe
salesman here.
Then he met Belle Finkelstein. "I
By Isabel Balotin
Harry Gendzier
Harry & Belle Gendzier
1979
In 1953, Sisterhood Presi¬
dent, Evelyn Mizrahi
presented Harry with a
large check for Sister¬
hood’s contribution to
the Building Fund.
took one look at her and that was the
end." After their long distance
courtship, they were married on
November 5, 1925, Harry’s 28th
birthday. "My wife and I are very
sentimental people," he says with
a smile. "We were engaged on her
birthday and married on mine."
During the depression, the
Gendziers owned a shoe business.
One day Harry Gendzier was having
trouble with the old car he drove. He
was talking with a man who was
driving a new sleek Buick. Harry
asked him what business he was in.
"The rag business", said the Buick
owner.
In 1937 Harry left the shoes
forever and with the barest of capital,
launched the Florida Rag Company,
a recycling business.
HARRY’S DREAM
All the while Harry was busy
making a living, he was very
concerned about the young Jewish
adults. As one of the 75 members of
the Orthodox Synagogue, B’nai
Israel. Harry felt that the restless
young adults of the congregation
were not able to enjoy the
overcrowded facilities. Harry
Gendzier, looking to the future, felt
as though Jewish life in Jacksonville
was deteriorating ... the attachment
remained with the synagogue only
for the High Holiday services;
births; deaths; marriages. He and
other congregants felt that it was
time for change. The synagogue
should hold the center of the cultural,
social and religious aspects of Jewish
life, ail under one roof. The
leadership was ready to go along
with the ideas. Rabbi Samuel
Benjamin, a rabbi of the
Conservative movement, was asked
to lead them toward Conservative
Judaism.
A house and lot was purchased on
the northwest corner of Third and
Silver Streets in Springfield, June 20,
1926. At that time there were 200
members of Congregation B’nai
Israel, and 125 pupils were enrolled
in Hebrew School. The ground¬
breaking for the new synagogue took
place June 5, 1927, and the congrega¬
tion celebrated its first holiday as
members of the Jacksonville Jewish
(continued on Page 21)

The Progressive Credit Union was established, and is operated to serve the financial needs of its members consisting of
men and women of the Jacksonville Jewish Community.
In 1979 we assisted many Jacksonville businessmen and families with loans for automobiles, appliances, marriages, and
other worthwhile personal needs. Under a special program designed to aid students with college tuition, Jewish students were
assisted in their higher education. The Student Loan Program is fully guaranteed by the U. S. Government.
New and innovative programs to help the financial needs of the Credit Union members have begun. New ideas are con¬
stantly being explored. Belonging to the Credit Union is an excellent form of savings. All members receive free Life Insurance
on their savings, up to $2,000.00, a special medium for savings. All loans are covered with free Credit Life Insurance for the full
outstanding balances. With all our extra services rendered, the ratio charged for interest is lower than that of other lending
institutions in the area. All savings are insured up to $40,000.00 by the Florida Credit Union Guarantee Company.
We extend a cordial invitation for all to join with us in the Progressive Credit Union to build a stronger Jewish community in
Jacksonville. For further information, please call Mr. Joe Cohen at 354-4770.
©os§ict ©a pdep ©ipele
for Jewish Ladies
Organized in 1960, Cassia Garden Circle, an affiliate of the Garden Club of Jacksonville, is a group of
Jewish women who are interested in gardening. Many of the charter members are still active in the group.
Cassia is a group of tropical trees, shrubs and plants with compound leaves, yellow flowers and long pods,
found in Israel. The Garden Club meets monthly. Programs have included Japanese Flower Arranging,
bonsai, hanging baskets, underwater arrangements, potpourri and landscaping. Many exciting plans have
been made for next year’s programming. Spend an interesting morning, and enjoy a light lunch with some
pleasant ladies. If you are interested call President, Tootsie Hecht at 398-5360.
Officers:
President Tootsie Hecht
1st Vice-President Jean Goodman
2nd Vice-President Helen Diamond
Corresponding Secretary Pearl Jarret
Secretary Marilyn Datz

River Garden Quxilliary
Mary Wolf - President
OFFICERS

1979-80 has been a “Banner Year” for the River Garden auxiliary. Many of our “impossible dreams”
have come true. Our membership has reached an all time high of over 1,000 with many new Life Members
included.
Our new volunteer program has proved most effective and has been a tremendous help to the
residents as well as the staff. Becoming a volunteer is the most important step one can take to help
River Garden. No one reaches so high as when he bends down to help a fellow human being. The act
of kindness in helping another gives one an indescribable feeling of pleasure and is the true meaning
of our motto “Service with a Heart”.
Through the efforts of an outstanding, dedicated board many innovations have been made possible
to enhance the lives of our residents. We have surpassed ourselves in fund raising. Tombola, Tree of Life,
Residents Memorial Plaque, Social Printing, Life Membership, and our highly successful new Donor
Program have helped the Auxiliary to provide money to purchase three whirlpools with elevators for
the health and comfort of our residents. Our fund raising has also been the source for extra-budgetary
needs of the Home including new furnishings, re-decoration and kitchen equipment.
One of our revitalized and popular programs has been the “Sing-A-Long” of Golden Oldies. Much
pleasure and satisfaction has been given our residents as they listen and sing and sometimes even dance
to their favorite tunes.
The Auxiliary strives to create a home-like atmosphere. Our goal is to fulfill the potential within
each of our residents. We try to accomplish this through sponsoring various programs and activities.
The Hobby Shop provides an outlet for the artist and the learning of many varied crafts. Both residents
of the Home and other senior citizens benefit from our sponsorship of the Golden Sunshine Club.
Holidays are always special occasions. The Auxiliary has a party and entertainment to celebrate
such special times as Chanukah, Purim, Mother’s and Father’s Day.
The talented reputation of our River Garden Players has spread far and wide. They are the highlight
of the year at our annual Linen Shower. Broadway may be calling any day.
We are entering a new decade of the 80’s and have high hopes that the New Year will be filled with
happiness, and our greatest wish that peace will be possible for all people.
725-4904

Message From The President
Dear Fellow Congregants:
It is with great honor for me to be asked to make this traditional
President’s message. It is also an opportunity to express my strong
feelings about our institution.
These strong feelings about the Jacksonville Jewish Center come from
a sense of history, a feeling of continuity through the generations. My early
memories of being at Third and Silver Streets with my grandfather,
Reverend Joseph Robin of blessed memory, underlie my present
involvement. A recent occurance reinforced this feeling of continuity
when I had the opportunity of removing the cornerstone of our old B’nai
Israel building as it was being razed. Behind the cornerstone was a copy of
the High Holiday service and a letter from the B’nai Israel building
committee of 1907, a letter that spanned across many years from our early leaders to our current leaders. The find
was most appropriate and meaningful to me.
The Center has progressed through several locations, Duval and Jefferson Streets, Third and Silver and 10101
San Jose Blvd., through several building programs. These moves and physical improvement programs were
invariably accompanied by fund-raising efforts. It will be a goal of my administration to complete and furnish our
synagogue-center facility.
In the same fashion as we have built and improved our physical plants through the years, our programs have
evolved from the earlier programs of our predecessors. The religious and educational efforts of earlier
administrations have made it possible for us to work toward the goals and needs of Conservative Judaism and our
own institution.
These program goals are being implemented in exemplary fashion by our dedicated and outstanding
professional staff. Few other institutions match our wide range of activities and all around efforts to serve our
members. It will be my job to continue these efforts.
We face the same problems as an institution as we do as individuals. Inflation, rising costs and energy problems
are as real to the Center as they are to all of us. My administration will need your help in working to keep our
budget funded and balanced.
Our commitment to Conservative Judaism and our own synagogue will benefit from the infusion of new
members we have added to our Board of Directors. I have much hope for our future when I think of the calibre of
the people who make up our Board.
My children brought Carolyn and me back into active participation in the mainstream of Jewish life. Our desire
for our children to have the best available Jewish education and exposure to Jewish life has brought us the great
dividend of re-involving ourselves. I must thank Carolyn and JoAnne, Charles and Miriam for supporting my
participation in Center leadership.
I appreciate the congregation’s confidence in me and will do my best. I look forward to much joy of
accomplishment and I hope for little frustration.
May we progress in the future as we have in the past.
L’ Shariah Tovah,
The Krestuls: Charles, Miriam, Nate, Carolyn and JoAnne

Lawrence Rackow
Executive Director
The Jewish Family & Children’s Services is the case work and counseling arm of the Jewish Community with service
for all age groups. Its goals, described in its by-laws, are to help individuals and families lead happier and healthier
lives, to enable individuals to approach their maximum potential, and to strengthen and/or restore family functioning
and stability.
In 1979,525 different individuals were served in one or more of its four programs — 281 in Counseling, 206 in Senior
Citizens’ Programs, 207 in Comprehensive Emergency Assistance, and 72 in the Resettlement Program.
Besides-the traditional services offered by Jewish social agencies, JFCS continued its outreach programs to the
patients at the State Hospital in McClenny and the prisoners at Raiford and Union County Correctional Institutions.
At the latter, we helped celebrate a PhD awarded one of the inmates. He is now editing a paper and heading a very
successful drug rehabilitation program there. He and a number of other prisoners are looking for pen pals. We also
have a weekly group at Mt. Carmel and a Friday afternoon group for isolated persons at River Garden where we
celebrated the graduation with high honors from FJC of a founding member. Our interest free college loans that we
secure from Jewish Children’s Services also reflects the Jewish stress on scholarship.
Florida’s enactment of a “point system” limiting medicare payments to the severely physically disabled requires
additional services to keep the aged and infirm in the community.
Our worker, Iris Goodstein, spent many hours helping to write and secure a large grant for a kosher meal program
sponsored by the Jewish Federation. We are attempting to secure funds to run a van to help senior citizens get
necessary medical care.
Our Resettlement Program, which resettled 9 in 1978, resettled 20 in fiscal year 1979, and set a goal of 30 for fiscal
year 1980. Fourteen of the 30 arrived by March and although there is hope that many will be released after the
Olympics, it is doubtful that we will reach the goal of 30. We have never had as many volunteers (139) so active and
gratified with their generous donations of their professional and human skills, time, effort and substance as in this
program. The New Americans have excellent self help and cultural groups. All 4 congregations have been
magnificent. We are particularly grateful to the Jewish Center in its camp and school scholarships and the one year
free membership offered to the newcomers. This is pouring over to raise the level of care for our local population,
especially the senior citizens. Our deepest appreciation to all the volunteers and an invitation for you to join us in our
most rewarding work.

===== PAGE 134 =====
Center on Hanukkah, December of
1927.
It was the Hebrew Junior League,
of which Harry was the first presi¬
dent, which spawned the idea of the
Center. It soon became the hub of
social, cultural and religious activities
for all age groups. For many years
Harry served as Chairman of the
Building fund. As a token of appre¬
ciation he was presented with a
silver cup and many plaques.
AN OUTSTANDING LEADER
Mr. Gendzier’s interest in the
welfare of the Jewish Community
has encompassed every field. He has
been a dominant figure in the growth
of the Jewish Community for over
six decades. He served as President
of the Jewish Community Council
(now known as the Jewish
Federation) in 1957 and 1958. To
this post and others he has held, he
has brought wisdom, vision and
direction. He has been a leader at
River Garden since its inception. His
expressive, outgoing manner, and
his alert mind made him a natural
spokesman for the group. At River
Garden he served as Admissions
Chairman from 1947-1951 and as
President from 1951-1956. Thejack-
sonville Jewish Center was most
fortunate to have Harry as its presi¬
dent. Many citations he has received
reflect his brilliant service to the
Center. Awards and plaques adorn the
walls of his home . . . each given for
his outstanding work on behalf of one
of the various organizations Harry has
led. As a symbol of his unselfish service,
Mr. Gendzier was awarded a place in
Who’s Who in World Jewry —
1955, when it was published for the
first time in history and has been in
ever since. As the book states,
"Selection is based upon self-
identification as a Jew as evidenced
by outstanding participation in some
phase of Jewish Life." In Mr.
Gendzier’s case, it well could have
been written "in every phase of
Jewish life."
On the National level, Harry
served as Vice-President of
United Synagogue of America
and as President of the South¬
east Region of United Synagogue.
In 1968, The Jewish Community
Council, the Jacksonville Jewish
Center and River Garden honored
Harry Gendzier for his 50 years of
service and dedication to
humanitarian causes.
HARRY AND BELLE
As a gift, Harry and Belle were
presented with a beautiful silver
plate and a trip to Israel. "I was so
thrilled I was almost overcome,"
Harry recalls. "I saw a land of milk
and honey. I saw fruit, produce,
vegetables being grown on land
Americans wouldn’t look at.”
To Harry and Belle the trip to
Israel was a dream come true but also
"a culmination of a lifestyle," Harry
remarks, "Judaism is a way of life.
Part of this way of life is to help your
fellow man." Known throughout the
Jewish community as a master fund¬
raiser, Harry modestly replies that
over the years he has raised millions
of dollars for worthy causes. "You
don’t live for yourself alone. I felt I
owed my fellow man part of my
patrimony. It is part of a pattern of
life. You don’t just live for yourself.”
The State of Israel Bond
Committee, the Jewish Theological
Seminary have also honored Harry
Gendzier. His business associates
and civic leaders have only the
highest regard for this charitable
man. A devoted family man, father to
Sheldon, and Rita, of blessed
memory, grandfather, and most of all
a loving husband to his supportive
and lovely wife, Belle, Harry
continues to be an inspiration to us
all.
HOPES FOR THE FUTURE
In a speech Harry gave at the
Israel Bond Dinner in his honor, he
said "Ideas must be born and carried
out fruitfully. We must teach our
fellow workers, not only that they
must carry on, but to improve their
performance. True leadership means
action by example, with an eye to
adding younger workers to take our
place." To Harry, "the strength of
the Jewish Community lies in the
synagogue. It is there as well as in the
home that we learn that Torah,
Avodah, and Tzedakeh have real
meaning. A vibrant synagogue must
urge its members to participate in all
worthwhile communal activities, but
it must also never lose it autonomy,
nor lost sight of its goals."
Now with his 83rd Birthday and
55th Wedding Anniversary just a
little over a month away, Harry and
his family are looking forward to
sharing this special Shabbat together
at the Jacksonville Jewish Center . . .
Harry’s Center.
JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER ACTIVITIES BUILDING
WHICH WAS ON THIRD & SILVER STREETS
"The Synagogue should hold the center of cultural, social, and religious
aspects of Jewish life all under one roof

ISRAEL KAPLAN LODGE NO. 800
B’nai B’rith
6161 Phillips Highway
Jacksonville, Florida 32216
(904) 731-3406
Picture of 1980-1981 Officers from left to right as follows:
Ronald Cohen, Treasurer; Richard Miller, Recording Secretary; Donald R. Sampson,
Executive Secretary; Stuart I. Hecht, President; Robert Armel, 1st Vice-President; Jon
Schlackman, 2nd Vice President; Milton, Hecht, Past President and Chaplain.
This has been a banner year for the Israel Kaplan Lodge No. 80. We have been involved in many successful community
projects. We participated in the Bell Ringer campaign for the Salvation Army at Christmas, a C.P.R. mannequin was donated to
the Red Cross, convalescent cards were sent to Jewish patients in local hospitals. We gave an award for outstanding service to a
local policeman. Lastly, and most important, as our main project for the year, we are donating a Torah to the Hillel House at
Florida State University.
An important step in lodge growth was achieved with the merger of the Wolfson Lodge with our lodge this year. We are
anxious to affiliate all interested Jewish men in our fine organization.
Much of the success of this year was due to our President, Milton Hecht and the fine group of officers who worked closely
with him. We expect the upcoming year to be even more successful and once again urge all jewish men to join and become
active.

TORAH READERS
Synagogue worship is a very democratic procedure. It enables
many with sufficient knowledge to lead the service and thus magnify
the glory of God in public. For those with the greater skill to read the
Torah which varies at each reading, it adds the dimension of public
education.
A new aspect of this program is the celebration of Bar, Bat Mitzvah
anniversaries, which reinforces the skills acquired during a year of
intensive training. A rewarding scene at these occasions is the
presence of members of the family who make a special effort to
attend this meaningful celebration.
We are, therefore, most happy that a number of talented pupils
have been added to the ranks of this select group, beginning with
their Bar Mitzvahs this year. They are:
Howard Markowitz Alan Mibab Roy Pomeranz
The program run at the Center may be the largest of its kind in
America. We have over three dozen people of all ages, who partic¬
ipate in the reading the year round, on festivals as well as Shabbat.
Ted Gross

CUB SCOUTS PACK 14
Front Row L-R: Jonathan Witten (mascot), Scott Balotin,
Mark Resnick, Colman Brodsky, David Silverman, Rachel
Mizrahi (mascot). Second Row L-R: Sharon Witten (Den
Leader) Kevin Levy, Jeff Ghelerter, Douglas Witten, Eric
Eisenberg, Alan Mizrahi, Judy Mizrahi (Den Leader). Not
pictured: Lee Pollock, Craig Kornblum, Richie Roey.
Webbo Leader Joel Garber, David Garber, Mathew
Hochman, David Lazarus, Kenny Haft, Sammy Stromberg.
Not pictured: Bobby Greenspoon and Jeff Swab.
L-R: Jonathan Weintraub, Brad Zimmerman,
Douglas Davis, Richie Davis, Steven Shapiro.
Not pictured: Den 1—Leon Jackler, Marty
Gottlieb, Jacob Sail, Dennis Mizrahi, Chip
Abolofia, Sean Usoff. Den 4—Ellis Mass,
Michael Gordon.
Den Leaders: Judy Davis & Lanny Zimmerman.
David Garber & Kenny Haft received the
Larry Hacker Award for scoring the highest
in attendance, achievements and good will in
Boy Scouting. Cub Master, Sidney Englehardt,
presented the award.
The highlight of the Scout Picnic was Blanche
Slott presenting Sidney Englehardt with a
momento (designed by Blanche) of his 17 years
in scouting. Hip, Hip, How! Hip, Hip,How!
Hip, Hip, How!
Cub Scouting, as an introduction to the Boy Scouts continues to be an outstanding youth program sponsored by the Jacksonville Jewish
Center. Boys between the ages of 8 and 10 find many new and challenging experiences in Cubbing.
The Cubs in each Den become a close unit as they work together to develop new hobbies, take field trips, explore nature and work
on craft projects and skits. The annual Rocket and Finewood Derbies give the boys the opportunity to design their own rockets and cars
and enter them in competitive racing.
In honor of Boy Scout Week the Cubs participated in Friday night Services and the annual Blue and Gold Banquet. This year Pack 14
received an honor ribbon for our participation in the Goodwill collection drive.
Cub Pack No. 14 is proud of its Cubmaster, Sidney Engelhardt, who has served in this capacity for 17 years. Chairman of our Pack
Committee is David Weintraub. Our very capable Den Leaders were Sharon Witten, Judy Mizrahi, Judy Davis, Judy Eisenberg, Lanny
Zimmerman, Eva Jackler with Joanne Davis serving as Den Leader Coach. Joel Garber led our Webelos Den.
The Webelos program is designed to fully prepare the ten year olds for their big step into Boy Scouting.
To close the year, members of Pack 14 and their families gathered at the Center Campsite for an afternoon of swimming and cooking over
an open fire. The highlight of the event was the presentation of the Larry Hacker Cub Scout Award to David Garber and Kenny Haft, the
Cubs scoring the highest in attendance, achievements and good will.

===== PAGE 154 =====
1980 GATORS A.Z.A. OFFICERS
President
Vice President
Treasurer ...
Secretary
... Jeff Harris
. RonBurman
Bob Grenadier
. Andy Mirkis
President Jeffrey Harris
I can hardly believe another year has passed. As Aleph S’gan (Vice-President) of last year, I played an active role
in our chapter, Gators A.Z.A. #272. In the past years we have had car washes, skating parties, beach parties,
athletic competitions, creative services and an abundance of many other enjoyable programs. During the first
weekend of May of 1980, we, along with our sister chapter, Gators B.B.G., hosted our annual Sweetheart-Heart-
throb Banquet, which not only attracted many local residents, but also appealed to our numerous guests from
as far as Daytona and Tampa. This program is always the big hit of the year. (Incidentally, Peri Kantor is 1980
Sweetheart and Adam Husney is 1980 Heartthrob.) In June we installed our 1980 officers and had our Life Cere¬
mony. (A program where some of our devoted seniors, who must leave, are given life membership to our chapter.)
As a result of our elections, I was elected Gators A.Z.A.’s proud new Aleph Godol (President).
For those of you who are not familiar with A.Z.A., it is an international organization created to develop mature
leadership qualities in Jewish teenage young men. All of our members become part of the A.Z.A. brotherhood.
We become a kind of growing family, consisting of many (and rapidly increasing) members. Each family member
has his own individual character and role, which further enhances the overall attitude of our chapter. In addition,
we have ‘relatives’ all over the world, whom we meet through the numerous B’nai B’rith Youth Organizations’
conventions. Thus originates our theme: “BBYO - A Wide World of Friends”.
Before closing, I must thank our devoted advisor, Ed Silverman. I encourage each and everyone of you to support
us, as we support you when we faithfully serve our community. If anyone is interested in joining us, and you are
a Jewish teenage male between grades 8 thru 12, then call either Ron Burman (268-0757), or myself (733-4315)
sometime real soon. SHALOM and HAVE THE HAPPIEST OF NEW YEARS! Fraternally yours Jeff Harris
Jewish War Veterans
LT. MEYER LIEBOVITZ POST 199
Jacksonville, Florida
■ JWV is more than a veterans organiza¬
tion. More than a Jewish organization. It is
an amalgamation of Americans of Jewish
faith who share the common experience of
having served their country in time of war.
■ JWV was founded in 1896 to counteract
slurs against Jewish patriotism. Since then,
it has been in the forefront of the battle
against anti-Semitism, bigotry and preju¬
dice. In upholding American democratic
traditions, JWV fights injustice and discrim¬
ination of all kinds.
■ JWV contributes greatly to the growth of
a sympathetic understanding of Israel and
its problems among all Americans. It is out¬
spoken on issues that are affecting per¬
manent peace in the Middle East. Likewise,
its leadership role continues to help arouse
American public opinion against Soviet
Union’s anti-Jewish policies.
■ JWV’s views are well known to Congress
on such diverse subjects as foreign policy,
civil rights, defense spending, veterans
benefits, housing and education.
■ JWV, through its hospital, rehabilitation
and veterans service programs, assists the
veteran and his dependents in many ways.
It maintains veteran service offices, manned
by professional experts, in major cities
throughout the country.
■ JWV, through its child welfare program,
supports the Boy Scout organization, pro¬
vides summer camp scholarships for under¬
privileged children, sponsors athletic league
tournaments. Its program includes sponsor¬
ship of brotherhood rallies and the under¬
taking of civic betterment projects in local
communities.

ADULT EDUCATION
The Jacksonville Jewish Center Adult Education program is designed to offer the members of our community the oppor¬
tunity to expand their knowledge of our history, religion and culture. The program continues to attract hundreds of people
from our community who want to engage in a program of Jewish study on a variety of levels. Courses in Hebrew, Jewish
philosophy, the Bible, Jewish Synagogue and Home skills, and a Bat Mitzvah program for women continue to be very suc¬
cessful.
The Minyannaire program sponsors many lecturers, some local and some of national notoriety. The Sisterhood
and the Library committee sponsor Shabbat morning Book reviews that are always very well attended.
Shabbaton Weekends this past year featured Max Dimont, a noted historian and author, Professor Mervin Verbit,
and Wolf Blitzer, the Washington correspondent for the Jerusalem Post. The weekends were very successful and stimulating.
Havurot or fellowships of groups, meet socially, within the intimacy of individual homes for the purpose of study, dis¬
cussion and celebration of Jewish life. New havurot are being formed all the time.
Weekly discussion of the “Portion of the Week” of the Torah along with a textual study of a tractate for the Talmud
take place every Shabbat before the afternoon Mincha service. The instructor is Mr. Jack Becker.
During the Shalosh Seudot meal following Minha, the Rabbi and Shabbat afternoon worshippers join in a study of
classical sources of Jewish literature.
Max I. Dimont
Noted historian and author of JEWS,
GOD AND HISTORY, THE
INDESTRUCTIBLE JEWS, AND
THE JEWS IN AMERICA.
Shabbaton Themes: “The Inde¬
structible Jews; The Secret of Jewish
Survival”. A “American Judaism,
Wasteland or Renaissance”.
Professor Mervin Verbit
Past President, Association for the
Sociological Study of Jewry, Consul¬
tant for the Center of Jewish
Community Studies. Associate
Professor, Tel Aviv University and
City University in New York.
Shabbaton Themes: “The Jewish
Family and Contemporary Culture”;
“Parents and Children—The Jewish
Family and Identity”; “Jewish Youth”;
“Coping—A Jewish Response to our
Social Ills”; “The Single and the Single
Parent”; “Problems of Loneliness and
Alienation in our Society”.
Wolf Blitzer
Washington Correspondent for the
Jerusalem Post. Sponsored by the
Zionist Organization of America
and the Minyanaires.
Themes: “U.S.-Israeli Relations
in Light of Camp David—an Over¬
View”, “The Carter-Begin-Sadat
Discussions, What Happened,
What Lies Ahead?”

The second Annual Jacksonville Jewish Center/Alexander Brest Tennis
Tournament was held during the week of December 2-9, 1979. Eleven teams
were entered in the men’s A/B division which was won by the team of Ronnie
Pollock/Rick Safier over Charlie Blalock/Bill Cohen. In the “C” Division, 12
teams were entered with the Dr. Steve Lazoff/Dr. Michael Scharf team coming
out on top over the team of Sherwin Burman/Ira Goldfield.
In all 46 men participated in good, healthy, fun-filled competition. Our
thanks go to the participants, Beauclerc Country Club and Big Tree Racquet
Club who donated valuable court time, Pic ’N Save who donated tennis balls
and the Tennis Committee of Ronnie Pollock, Arthur Goldstein, Neil Sandler,
Shelley Kulchin and Debbie Goldstein who gave of their valuable time.
A special thanks to Mr. Alexander Brest who so graciously donated the
tennis courts at the Center and in whose honor this tournament is held.
We are looking forward to the Third Annual Tournament which should
be the best yet.
Ira Goldfield, Athletics Chairman

Holocaust Commemoration
and
Israel Independence Day Celebration
A very important and ambitious program was planned in com¬
memoration of the Holocaust and the celebration of Yom Haatzmaut,
Israel’s Independence Day. The program was called “From Darkness
to Light” to underscore the theme of light and hope with which Jews
faced life. As tragic as the holocaust was, it gave way nevertheless to
the greatest contemporary miracle, the restoration and building of
Israel.
The four congregations and their rabbis participated in the day’s
events, which included a poster contest, music, dance, poetry and
dramatic presentation by the 9th grade Hebrew High Class of “The
Fighters of the Warsaw Ghetto.”
Entrance to the Darkness to Light Program
“Fighters of the Warsaw Ghetto”
This play tells of the heroism and resistance of the Warsaw Ghetto
fighters. Left to right: Jeff Harris, Cary Zel, Dorone Manasse,
Blair Selber, Michael Freedman, Lane Strumlauf, Marcy Green-
spoon, Caryn Rosenthal, Janet Jacobson.
9th Grade Hebrew High
Dana Levitan, Director of the “Fighters of the Warsaw Ghetto”
poses with actors before rehearsals.

They tell of Adam:
How frightened he must have been when
for the first time
he saw the sun disappear,
ending the light of day.
It was Adam’s first darkness!
How could he understand the night,
when he had never seen a dawn ?
After the splendor of the sun,
how astonishingly dark the darkness was;
how desperate the long terror of the first fall of night...
until Adam learned day would come again:
that there is order in the universe.
And he could begin to see
how much light remains in the sky at night:
the stars, and their enduring promise of the sun.
The returning star of day.
Adam learned
the night is never wholly dark, and no night is endless...
even as each of us must learn it
in our own times of trouble and of darkness.
The light is never far.

«ENTE* OAy CAM¡>
The Center Day Camp has come a long way since its beginning in 1946 as Camp Yom Tov, an extension of the
afternoon Hebrew School. Facilities included the use of the Gym and classrooms in the former Center at Third
and Silver Streets and the adjacent park. Motel swimming pools and a portable pool in back of the building served
for a swim program.
Today we are proud to offer the Jacksonville Jewish community the only Jewish Day Camp with an extensive
program for children from 2l/2 years of age through our oldest group of 13-15 year olds who take part in the
Counselor-in-Training program.
This summer the utilization of the air conditioned building for the Camp office, Gym and nursery rooms plus the
involvement of Day School teachers proved to be most successful. Outside facilities included an Olympic
swimming pool, tennis courts, softball and soccer fields, vollyball, tether ball, playground and shaded camping and
assembly areas. A Kosher snack bar with an excellent cook provided nutritional lunches served in the large
covered gazebo and picnic area. Snacks were served to half day campers each morning and to the entire staff in
the afternoon.
An outstanding staff of specialists provided skilled and experienced leadership in creative programming. Judaic
activities were very ambitious with “Shabbat” and the culmination of the theme of the week in song, dance and
drama (to which parents were invited) each Friday, The themes included the portrayal of “Jewish Roots”, “Jews
Around the World”, a talent show and others.
More highlights included “Star Wars, the Emperor Strikes Back”, two trips to Kingsley Beach, overnight
camping each session and the big overnight trip to Gold Head State Park. Each session also included full length
movies complete with cartoons and refreshments. (“The Love Bug”, “Cat from Outer Space”, “Gus”, “That
Darned Cat” and “The Computer That Wore Tennis Shoes”.)
Our swimming program included Red Cross instruction with an introduction to water for the young campers,
beginner, intermediate, advanced swimming and lifesaving. The wide variety of other activities kept campers busy
with horseback riding, all sports (indoor and outdoor), arts and crafts, nature, overnight camping and field trips.
Thanks to our staff of specialists, counselors and CIT’s, our top programming in all areas kept a steady
enrollment of over 100 campers throughout the entire summer.
A special thanks to all the parents who shared their children with us, for without you there could be no camp.
We look forward to your support for continued growth.
Blanche Slott, Administrative Director
Judy Reppert, Program Director
Edna Naveh-Judiac Story Time
Frisbee-Popular Sport

beginner swimmers

NEAL F. PORTNOY YOUTH LOUNGE
Mrs. Louis Portnoy cuts ribbon at dedication Rabbi Gaffney, Rabbi Kentof & Jerry Zel, Co-Chairman of the Youth
of Neal Portnoy Youth Lounge Committee & Eliot Safer, Chairman of the day, participate in the
dedication.
The Neal F. Portnoy Youth Lounge celebrated its formal opening on Sunday, November 4th at 12:00 noon. The youth
lounge is named in loving memory of Neal F. Portnoy, which was made possible through the generosity of the Louis Portnoy
family. A well equipped and well designed facility to serve the needs of the youth of our congregation and the Jewish youth of the
entire community was one of the abiding concerns that animated Lou Portnoy before his untimely death. His wife, our beloved
Goldie, who shared her late husband’s concerns that “our young people are our future” generously provided for the furnishings,
games and hobby activities which will make our youth lounge one of the most attractive of its kind.
Under the direction of the Youth Commission and most specifically George Strumlauf, Jerry Zel, Horty Gendzier, Rhoda
Goldstein, Sheila Jacobson and with the guidance of Bud Shorstein, Chairman of the Board, and Rabbi Dov Kentof, the youth
lounge was furnished for every conceivable use by the youth for every situation.
The Neal F. Portnoy Youth Lounge will be used by our youth in a variety of ways. It will be the central location for formal
meetings, programs and activities. It will also serve as the synagogue when youth services are held. There will also be a schedule
when Jewish children of the entire community will be able to gather informally and use the lounge as a “drop-in” center. Equipped
with all sorts of games, such as pinball machines, foozball, bumper pool games, electronic and board games, the youth lounge will
have something for everybody. The opening of the Neal F. Portnoy Youth Lounge served to highlight the commitments that our
Center has made to the youth of our congregation and to the youth of the community. It has always been our central purpose to
create an environment in which Jewish children are provided the programs and activities they need in a setting conducive to
growth as individuals, as Jews, and as human beings. Having committed itself to these ends, the congregation is deeply grateful
to the Louis Portnoy family for making the youth lounge one more facility which will enable us to realize these goals.
The opening celebration for the Youth Lounge was highlighted by brief remarks of tribute to the Portnoy family, the cutting
of a ribbon, the hanging of a mezuzah purchased by Karen Mizrahi, Nathan Slaff and Sammy Jacobson in Israel and the opening
of the facility for use for young people. Characteristically, the congregation will host a program for teenagers and the entire
community in the Neal F. Portnoy Youth Lounge. There could be no more fitting Amen to a life of concern for young people
which characterizes the family of Louis Portnoy than the sounds of happy and excited youth using a facility named in the memory
of their own son. Even more fitting, this will be a tribute to them throughout the months and years that the Neal F. Portnoy Youth
Lounge is so used.

U. S. Y
The general aim of the United Synagogue Youth program is to bring about a meaningful and fully recip¬
rocal encounter of Judaism, the Jewish people, and the synagogue on one side, and the Jewish teenager
on the other. As a result of this encounter the Jewish teenager will, firstly, demonstrate and experience
how the Jewish way of living, through study, action and fellowship leads the way to personal fulfillment
and growth while meeting his spiritual and social needs, and secondly make a significant contribution to
his synagogue, community, society and the Jewish people.
U.S.Y. Officers
L-R: Joy Pollock, Sabrina Katz, Laura Abramson,
Joanne Krestul, Daniel Klausner, David Katz, Jodi Roey.

The Chalutzim, Pioneers, are entering a new year with exciting and dynamic programming. Some of

What are the 3
most widely held securities
in the United States?
U. S. Savings Bonds,
A. T. & T. . . . and
STATE OF ISRAEL BONDS!
You can’t argue with success, as the saying goes. And Israel Bonds
have had a lot of success since their inception in 1951. Over 350
of the largest banks and financial institutions in the United States own
Israel Bonds. Almost $4 billion worth have been sold, of which $2 billion
has been repaid as they came due.
State of Israel bond money never leaves the United States. The
proceeds remain here in the form of bank credits, permitting Israeli
industrialists and agricultural experts to purchase equipment and
machinery to create jobs for newcomers to Israel, who are arriving
by the hundreds each week.
There are dozens of ways you can use Israel bonds other
than in your investment portfolio . . .
Gifts to Universities: every major college in the United States has
accepted Israel Bonds. If you choose Harvard, they’ll match your gift
with an equal Israel Bonds purchase.
Use Israel bonds instead of cash for travel in Israel.
Pension Plans: Many pension fund and Keogh Plan retirement funds
use Israel Bonds as investments.
Mr. Jack Becker will be honored at the Israel Bond Dinner on
Tuesday, October 7, 1980.

Rabbi’s Message
“HALLEL”
The cover which graces our Year Book this year depicts the beautiful bas relief created by Ami
Shamir, the artist we have commissioned to design our synagogue interior. The work is called “Hallel” or
“Praise”. Most of us recognize the word from the psalmist’s expression “Hallelujah”... “Praise in worship,
the Lord”. This proposed work of art will be prominently and fittingly displayed in the lobby or promenade
facing the entrance to the Center. It is a fitting place and theme for a synagogue entry for it suggests that at
the heart ... at the very core of our activities, as Center members, as worshippers, as Jews, yes, even as
human beings, is the ability to give expression to a sense of reverence, awe and wonderment of life,
creation, and of existence itself.
The quotation, cited on the cover is from the 146th psalm. It carries the message which accompanies
this work of art. The expression conveys the worshipful attitude, “I praise the Lord with my life, I shall sing
to God with all my being”. This verse is recited by worshipping Jews each morning. It proposes that,
ultimately, happiness, joy and contentment is found in that heart and soul which senses that it has been
blessed beyond all measure of comprehension. It suggests that we as individuals make the following
blessing .. . “Praised are You, O Lord, King of the Universe, You ordain the order of all creation, You cause
light to shine over the earth and bring peace to its inhabitants”. The rabbis propose that “those who cannot
bring themselves to make this blessing are as the wicked, who are dead even in their lifetime, for they see
the sun rising without praising God”. The tradition really means to say that a person is dead spiritually if he
has no delight, if he cannot sense the mystery of the rising and setting of the sun, in the bread that he eats
and the love with which he has been blessed.
The Biblical words about the Genesis of Heaven and earth are not words of information, they are
words of wonder and thanksgiving. Abraham Heschel warned that mankind will not perish for want of
information but for want of appreciation. The story of
creation is not a description of how the world came into
being, but a song that there is a world at all and that there
is a Reality that sustains it. “And God saw that it was
good” (Gen. 1). This is the challenge: To reconcile God’s
view of the world with our experience.
Rabbi David and Inge Gaffney,
Shalva and Asher
This is also our challenge as we enter the New Year
of 5741. It is a challenge which addresses itself to our
leadership, to our worshippers and to the members of
our congregation. “Hallel” proposes that our life is an act
of faith on the part of God. God, who sees us waste so
much . . . our time, our life and our love, nevertheless
continues to give us new time and new life. Our life then
must be worthy of this trust. Our love of Him must be a
response to His love of us which is manifest with each
new breath, each new day and each new year. Every
moment represents God’s faith in us. The way we live
throughout the year is our response to His faith. To be
worthy of God’s expectations, to “praise God with my
life, to sing to Him with my being” (Psalm 146) is the task
of human existence. May we prove worthy of this task.
Inge and I, Asher and Shalva, join in wishing you and
yours a life of health, of joy, of meaning and of purpose.
Rabbi David Gaffney

===== PAGE 178 =====
RAFT TRIP
Troop 14, in its 59th year of existence explored new frontiers this
year. The Troop, one of the oldest in North Florida, completed the rugged,
six day Suwannee River Raft trip for the first time in history. Appreciation
goes to the Berman Brothers, Inc. for their assistance in the design and
construction of the raft.
Boys from Troop 14 also completed the lengthy, historic St. Augustine
Trail Hike for the national award.
Highlights of this past year included: the annual Ross Allen Fall
Encampment at which Troop 14 captured one of four blue ribbons awarded
all North Florida troops; Scout Sabbath, featuring guest speaker, Harold
Clark, Circuit Judge; the annual Blue and Gold Banquet; the District
Camporee and various campouts.
Scouts learned what it is like to be behind bars when they toured
the Duval County Jail as guests of the Jacksonville Sheriff’s Department.
Service projects included presentatiion of a magic show at River
Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged, and maintenance and repairs for
the residents of Hubbard House for Battered and Abused Women. Both
projects were conducted under the direction of Irving Sucholeiki.
Scoutmaster Randall Silverberg
Assistant Scoutmaster William R. Wilensky
Jr. Assistant Scoutmaster GaryKupfer
Senior Patrol Leader Josh Weiss
. . . More Campers

Yeladim

JUNIOR CONGREGATION
Modeled after the Senior Congregation, our Junior Congregation is an organization of all students in classes.
The main purpose is to conduct Shabbat morning classes and services in their own place of worship, led by their
own advisors and the children themselves. We have found that children who attend Shabbat morning services not
only become familiar with the service and feel comfortable attending, but have an opportunity to read Hebrew
extensively and to put into practice that which is taught during the week. Shabbat School is considered a part of
our Hebrew School program.
Junior Congregation invades Disney World!!!
Bruce Gelman, David Garber & Jonathan DePaul
enjoy Tomorrowland.

===== PAGE 180 =====
The Sunday School of the Jacksonville Jewish Center is planned expressly
for children in Kindergarten, First and Second Grades. A two hour program provides
each child the opportunity to experience the richness of their Jewish Heritage
through song, dance, role playing, cooking, short stories, games and arts and crafts.
Emphasis is placed on the holidays and festivals. Jewish values and mitzvot are
encouraged.
This year the Sunday School program was co-ordinated by Tricia Bloom.
The staff included Nancy Minkoff, kindergarten teacher; Scott Zimmerman, first
grade; and Shirley Bielski, second grade. Judy Geller served as music specialist,
teaching Hebrew songs to the children, and Tricia Bloom served as arts and
crafts specialist.

when told by Scott Zimmerman.

WHAT IS SZECHUAN CUISINE?
Szechuan Cuisine is a brilliant freak which breaks all the rules of conventional Chinese Mandarin cooking and gets away
with it. The use of relishes and seasonings is another language. The manifold flavour is the chief characteristic of Szechuan
food. Many dishes are simultaneously sour, sweet, hot, salty, and aromatic. One is often surprised by the first taste, but
after awhile one can see that there is a different logic that makes Szechuan cuisine so unique and colorful in character that
it stands out as one of the world’s best cuisines.
Ask manager Hok Leung to explain the delightful difference of Szechuan Gardens. However you prefer your Chinese
dishes, (our special hot & spicy ... or mild ... or sweet) we will gladly prepare for you!
SZECHUAN GARDENS Chinese Restaurant
Each Dish is carefully prepared just the way You Want It!

Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held daily,
morning and evening in the Witten Chapel. Friday Family Services,
Sabbath and Holiday Services are observed throughout the entire
year.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish customs
are observed in connection with Circumcision, Naming of Girls,
Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat Mitzvah, Confirmation, Marriages,
Conversion to Judaism, Bereavement.
Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery with outstanding
program of secular and religious content. Hebrew school, carefully
graded with intensive Hebrew content, including Jewish history
and customs, Sunday School for kindergarten, first and second
grades: Solomon Schechter Day School, a graded secular and Hebrew
School, 1st through 6th grades; Hebrew High School with Pre-Jr.
and Junior Congregations and Minyanaire.
/
Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection of juvenile
and adult Jewish books; Adult Education, religious and cultural
Jewish courses; music programs of religious and cultural Jewish
music; P.T.A.; Sisterhood and Ha’Rayim, a fellowship of young
families; social activities provided by Mr. and Mrs. Club, Single
Adults; Family Recreation Program (Olympic pool), summer week¬
days and Sundays for all ages.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth; United Synagogue
Youth, various age levels are served by Center Youth League,
Charles Palot Club, Kadima, Boy Scouts, Cub Scouts, Girl Scouts,
Brownies; Sunday Funday; athletics, programs for youth and adults;
Center Day Camp and summer program at our 36 acre site on San
Jose Blvd.
Family Recreation Program — Center Campsite . . . swimming,
athletics, snack bar; available for members during the summer
months.
For additional information, call the Center office - 268-6736.

MOUNT CARMEL GARDENS
1980-81 OFFICERS
Mount Carmel Gardens
Tenants Association
Officers of the Tenants Association, for
1980-81: President-Mary Foor, 1st Vice-
Dottie Levine, 2nd Vice-Ruth Blanc, 3rd
Vice-Lona Issac, Treasurer-George
Blitzer, Corres. Sec’y.-Isabelle Linden,
Recording Sec’y.-Mildred Shafner.
Mount Carmel Gardens is conveniently located at University Boulevard and Carmel Terrace, next door
to a synagogue, and across the street from a shopping center, where almost any need can be purchased. The
city bus stops at the corner,-there are doctors, beauty parlors and any kind of restaurant one could want in
the immediate vicinity.
Our 208 apartment high-rise is filled with seventeen floors of friendly people. We have continuous social
and educational activities, and the retirees who live here are assured of security, comfort, safety,
independence, and the enrichment of stimulating social-recreational activities shared with others.
Our Tenants Association has at its head a very efficient group of officers and board members, whose
goal is to bring more projects into our schedule, to benefit the folks living here, the latest of these is the
procurement of the use of a bus for the benefit of those needing transportation to the doctor, the hospital, for
emergency or other use. Also, the AARF has formed a chapter based in our building, from which everyone
can benefit. Pending is the formation of a nutrition center nearby, so that all who are in need or who are
interested may have a noon-day meal.
There is a Newsletter distributed free every month containing all the “news-that’s-fit-to-print” of our
activities, our tenants and our building.
Our Carmel Singers and Carmel Entertainers are always “on the go” -- to put on their programs for
anyone requesting them, -and many a convalescent home has had some pleasure added to the lives of the
“wheel-chair” audience by these performances. The “Entertainers” were the only group selected by the
Mayor for his May-Day celebration (where our Fannie Kaufman was selected Senior May-Day Queen)-and
for his “Fishathon”, held at Burt Maxwell Park. Fannie Kaufman always plays accompaniment to any of our
entertaining activities.
At our Senior Citizens home, occupied by both Jews and Gentiles, all the apartments are equipped with
an emergency button, which brings immediate help for anyone who needs it-and a smoke and fire alarm
system is installed on every floor. Here, all are contented and congenial neighbors-living in our comfortable
and enjoyable Mount Carmel community.
Pauline Schemer
The Carmel Singers performing at the
Arlington Lion’s Club.
Fannie Kaufman, chosen Senior May-Day
Queen at the Mayor’s Senior Citizens’
May-Day celebration-and Mary Foor, 1st
Runner-up.
Pauline and Israel Schemer in costume,
before joining the other tenants at their
Hawaiian Dinner Dance.
Milton Foor’s band playing for the “thank
you” dance, hosted by Mary Foor.

CEMETERY COMMITTEE
OF THE
Jacksonville Jewish Center
KENNETH BOWDEN
General Manager
JACK BECKER
Co-Chairman
JOE BECKER
Co-Chairman
The Cemetery Committee provides a very necessary function of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. It is very important that
the Cemetery grounds are well kept and provide a peaceful resting place for our loved ones.
Our Cemetery Committee is co-chaired by Joe Becker and Jack Becker. Serving on their committee are Max Becker,
Hymie Selber, Alan Taffet, Dr. Joe Witten, Dr. Sam Witten, Jack Bettman and Joel Bressler.
Mr. Kenneth A. Bowden, Controller of the Jacksonville Jewish Center, also serves as general manager of our Cemetery. It
is his responsibility to oversee the upkeep and maintenance of the grounds. He has an excellent working relationship with
Mr. Jennewine who is general manager of Evergreen Cemetery. Mr. Jennewine has been very generous with his time and his
staff, Mr. Campbell, Mr. Miller and Mrs. Phillips. They have all taken excellent care of the Center’s property, above and beyond
what is expected. We appreciate everything they do.
Johnny B. Mercer has been custodian of our Cemetery for over 20 years. Mr. Bergerson of Southern Monument Co., has
always been extremely helpful to us, too. We are very grateful to all these people who have given so generously of their time.
